                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Nitrogen+Doped&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_nitrogen%20doped">nitrogen doped</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Carbon+Doped&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_carbon%20doped">carbon doped</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Oxygen+Vacancy&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_oxygen%20vacancy">oxygen vacancy</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Battery&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20battery">sodium-ion battery</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Capacitor&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20capacitor">sodium-ion capacitor</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have received great attention from all over the world since they came out. The lack of lithium resources has gradually emerged with the rapid development of new energy industries; thus, researchers have turned more attention to sodium metal, which has more abundant resource reserves and lower cost. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 1 to 3">(1â3)</a> Therefore, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered to be a powerful alternative to traditional LIBs, especially in large-scale energy storage. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4 ref5 ref6'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5 ref6" aria-label="reference 4 to 6">(4â6)</a> Unfortunately, commercial LIB anode materials (graphite) have very poor sodium storage performance due to the large radius and high ionization potential of sodium ions. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7 ref8'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref8" aria-label="reference 7 and 8">(7,8)</a> Hence, it became an urgent problem to be solved for the development of anode materials for SIBs with high energy density and long service life. Also, sodium-ion capacitors (SICs) have also offered another route for electrochemical energy storage devices. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9" aria-label="reference 9">(9)</a> Different from traditional battery and capacitor mechanisms, SICs are usually composed of the battery-type anode and the capacitor-type cathode, which will make SICs present different working mechanisms at both ends. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10 ref11'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref11" aria-label="reference 10 and 11">(10,11)</a> The key to the construction of high-performance SICs is to find a suitable cathode and anode for kinetics matching. Activated carbon (AC), has excellent pseudocapacitance performance, is usually used as the cathode of SICs. This requires the matching anode material to also have fast sodium storage kinetics. Among various types of anode materials for SIBs and SICs, alloying materials are accompanied by larger volume expansion during the cycling, resulting in electrode pulverization and capacity attenuation; <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12 ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref13" aria-label="reference 12 and 13">(12,13)</a> conversion materials have obvious voltage hysteresis and low initial coulomb efficiency (ICE). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14" aria-label="reference 14">(14)</a> The insertion/extraction reaction type materials, such as hard carbon, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> molybdenum-based material, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> titanium-based oxides, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10" aria-label="reference 10">(10)</a> have received great attention due to their good structural stability, kinetics, and suitable sodium-ion intercalation voltage as candidate anode materials for SIBs and SICs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17 ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref18" aria-label="reference 17 and 18">(17,18)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Titanium dioxide (TiO<sub>2</sub>) is widely used in photocatalysis, solar cells, and energy storage systems as a well-known semiconductor material due to the abundant reserves, low cost, and environmental friendliness. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19 ref20 ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19 ref20 ref21" aria-label="reference 19 to 21">(19â21)</a> At the same time, it has also attracted great attention from researchers due to the suitable sodiation voltage and zero-strain characteristics since TiO<sub>2</sub> was first used as the anode material for SIBs in 2011. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> It exhibits high theoretical specific capacity (335 mA h g<sup>â1</sup>) and outstanding structural stability during cycling, but the application is also limited by low conductivity and ion diffusivity due to the shortage of d electrons in TiO<sub>2</sub> polymorphs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> Therefore, many solutions have been proposed to solve the above-mentioned problems. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24 ref25 ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref25 ref26" aria-label="reference 24 to 26">(24â26)</a> Defect engineering, such as oxygen vacancies (OVs) and heterogeneous element doping (C, N, P, S, B, F, and Sn), is a very effective means to improve the electrochemical performance of metal oxides among the many modification methods. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31 ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31 ref32" aria-label="reference 27 to 32">(27â32)</a> The OVs can enhance the electronic and ionic conductivity and the sodium storage capacity of metal oxides. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25 ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref33" aria-label="reference 25 and 33">(25,33)</a> At the same time, defective nanomaterials can also greatly enhance the capacitance performance, which has also been proven to be an effective method to overcome weak sodium ion diffusion. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34 ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34 ref35" aria-label="reference 34 and 35">(34,35)</a> For example, Mai et al. successfully synthesized anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> rich in OVs by the spray-pyrolysis-assisted method and demonstrated up to 2000 cycles at a current density of 1 A g<sup>â1</sup> as the anode materials of SIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> Ji et al. reported N,C-codoped yolk-like TiO<sub>2</sub>, which showed a long life span of up to 3000 cycles. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> Wu et al. introduced N and OVs in TiO<sub>2</sub> by ex-electrospinning, and the double doping strategy made it exhibit remarkable sodium storage performance. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> However, the low solubility and diffusion rate of the external dopants cause the doping depth to be limited to near the surface of TiO<sub>2</sub>, which may harm the electron transfer and weaken the advantages of doping. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> Many strategies have been proposed to increase the doping concentration. Gan et al. proposed a defect-assisted phosphorus doping strategy, the reduced TiO<sub>2</sub> with rich OVs and Ti<sup>3+</sup> species enhanced the diffusion of dopants, and finally obtained high-concentration phosphorus-doped (7.8 at %). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> However, a high concentration of OVs will significantly reduce the diffusion rate of Li<sup>+</sup> in TiO<sub>2</sub>, resulting in a lower capacity at high current densities, which has been reported and confirmed by many works of literature. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39 ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39 ref40" aria-label="reference 39 and 40">(39,40)</a> The diffusion barrier of Li<sup>+</sup> is 0.56 eV via density functional theory (DFT) calculations when the concentration of OVs in TiO<sub>2</sub> is low, which is significantly lower than the diffusion barrier in perfect TiO<sub>2</sub> (0.63 eV). As the concentration of OVs increases, the diffusion barrier increases to 1.17 eV. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> Therefore, an in-depth understanding of the properties of heteroatom-doped TiO<sub>2</sub> from the perspective of balanced electron and ion migration is particularly important for achieving high sodium storage capacity and a long-term cycle life. Compared with single heteroatom doping, the research on multi-atom doping to promote the sodium storage capacity of TiO<sub>2</sub> is still not thorough enough. Recently, Fan et al. prepared TiO<sub>2</sub> doped with N and S and showed strong sodium storage performance. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> However, the use of dopants for post-treatment of TiO<sub>2</sub> to achieve heteroatom doping still does not maximize the effect of doping. Moreover, the synergistic effect of heteroatom doping and OVs on the enhanced sodium storage behavior of TiO<sub>2</sub> has not been further discussed. Therefore, a full understanding of the synergy between double heteroatom doping and OVs has significant guiding significance for the design and preparation of high-performance anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> anodes.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Here, we design and synthesis N, C co-doped hierarchical TiO<sub>2</sub> microspheres covered by ultrathin nanosheets with rich OVs (CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub>). This strategy consists of the synthesis of TiO<sub>2</sub> microspheres through the Oswald ripening route and a low-cost annealing process to achieve abundant OV doping. This unique electrode design has many benefits. First, the wide interlayer spacing and ultra-thin nanosheets provide a shorter and faster sodium ion diffusion path. Second, the N and C co-doping reduces the band gap of TiO<sub>2</sub>, while the presence of OVs efficiently accelerates the diffusion of sodium ions. As a result, the CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub> microsphere anode exhibited high specific capacity (245 mA h g<sup>â1</sup> after 3000 cycles at 0.1 A g<sup>â1</sup>), superior cycling stability (91 mA h g<sup>â1</sup> after 10,000 cycles at 10 A g<sup>â1</sup>), and excellent rate capability (97 mA h g<sup>â1</sup> at 10 A g<sup>â1</sup>). In addition, CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub>//Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C-BN (NVP@C-BN) sodium-ion full-batteries (SIFBs) and CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub>//AC SICs were fabricated to demonstrate the promising application of CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub>. The SIFBs present a specific capacity of 115 mA h g<sup>â1</sup> after 2000 cycles at 1 A g<sup>â1</sup>. The SICs deliver a maximum energy density of 174.2.1 W h kg<sup>â1</sup> and a maximum power density of 12,500 W kg<sup>â1</sup>. Theoretical calculation further demonstrates that the introduction of N, C atoms and OVs into hierarchical TiO<sub>2</sub> promotes the electron/ion dynamics and enhances the sodium storage performance of the material.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">This product was obtained through an Oswald ripening route and subsequent hydrogenation process, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/suppl_file/am1c10506_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>, and the time evolution method was used to verify the formation mechanism of TiO<sub>2</sub> (Figure S2, see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/suppl_file/am1c10506_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a> for details). <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/suppl_file/am1c10506_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a> shows the  X-ray diffraction (XRD) spectrum of all samples. The TiO<sub>2</sub> sample exhibited a weak diffraction peak, indicating its poor crystallinity before calcination. Note that the remaining two samples show strong diffraction peaks after calcination. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/suppl_file/am1c10506_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S4</a>, and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/suppl_file/am1c10506_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a> show the Rietveld refinements results of CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub> and CNâTiO<sub>2</sub>; they all show a very slight change in the lattice constant, and it may be caused by defects in the crystals. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/suppl_file/am1c10506_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a> shows the X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) survey spectra of all samples, which indicates the successful introduction of the N and C species into the TiO<sub>2</sub>. As shown in the scanning electron microscope (SEM) images in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b,c, the surface of the CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub> microspheres was rough and had an average diameter of 700 nm, which was the same as TiO<sub>2</sub> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/suppl_file/am1c10506_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2f</a>). A large number of cracked microspheres can be seen, and the hierarchical structure was also revealed, which can also be seen from the transmission electron microscope (TEM) image. The microsphere surface was covered by ultra-thin nanosheets interlaced to form a porous structure, which was conducive to electron transport and electrolyte penetration. The enlarged TEM image shows that the interlayer spacing of the nanosheet was about 0.63 nm. The high-resoluton TEM (HRTEM) image (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>d) taken at the edge of the nanosheet was used to determine the crystal plane and composition, and the plane spacing of the ordered crystal lattice was measured to be close to 0.35 nm, corresponding to the (101) plane of the anatase crystal phase. The selected area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern proves that the material was polycrystalline and has a good degree of crystallinity (inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>d). The elemental mapping displays the uniform distribution of C, N, Ti, and O, which further confirmed the composition and distribution of the element (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>e). <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/suppl_file/am1c10506_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a> shows the SEM and TEM images of CNâTiO<sub>2</sub>, which are consistent with TiO<sub>2</sub> and CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub>, indicating that the heat treatment process did not damage the morphology.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/asset/images/medium/am1c10506_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/asset/images/large/am1c10506_0002.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) XRD pattern of CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub>. (b) SEM, (c) TEM, (d) HRTEM (inset: SAED pattern), and (e) HADDFâSTEM images and the elemental distribution of C, N, Ti, and O of CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/asset/images/large/am1c10506_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c10506&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The samples were further characterized by XPS analysis. The existence of nitrogen elements in three samples was further confirmed by the high-resolution N 1s spectrum (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a). The peaks located at 399 and 400 eV can be attributed to the pyrrolic N and pyridinic N, and it has been demonstrated that the defect sites are caused by pyrrolic N and pyridinic N are beneficial for enhancing the electronic conductivity of carbon. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41 ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41 ref42" aria-label="reference 41 and 42">(41,42)</a> Quantitative analysis shows that the atomic ratios of nitrogen in TiO<sub>2</sub>, CNâTiO<sub>2</sub>, and CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub> are 0.78, 2.12, and 2.21%, respectively. The high-resolution C 1s spectrum in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b demonstrate that the peaks at 284.68, 285.48, and 288.58 eV are assigned to sp<sup>2</sup> CâC, CâO, and CâO bonds of carbon species, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> and at 283.98 eV is associated with the CâTi bond for CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> Note that the CâO bond is not observed in TiO<sub>2</sub>, and the signal of the CâTi bond in CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub> is enhanced simultaneously; they both play an important role in stabilizing the OV center of the TiO<sub>2</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c, the peak at 529.8 eV of CNâTiO<sub>2</sub> originated from TiâOâTi shifts to lower binding energies of 529.4 eV for CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub>, the peak at 531.3 eV can be identified as the presence of surface functional groups (âOH) or OVs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a> In the high-resolution Ti 2p spectrum of the CNâTiO<sub>2</sub> and CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d), the Ti<sup>4+</sup> species with Ti 2p<sup>3/2</sup> and 2p<sup>1/2</sup> signals at 458.58 and 464.56 eV for CNâTiO<sub>2</sub> are shifted to 458.34 and 463.66 eV for CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub>, respectively. An additional component is deconvoluted at 463.87 eV for CNâTiO<sub>2</sub> and 463.66 eV for CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub>, ascribed to Ti<sup>3+</sup>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a> The bathochromic shift phenomenon of O 1s and Ti 2p spectra confirmed together the successful introduction of TiâC bond and OVs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> Quantitative analysis showed that the atomic ratios of Ti and O in CNâTiO<sub>2</sub> and CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub> samples were 23.25 and 46.07 at % and 26.76 and 51.59 at %, respectively, which also proved that there are more OVs in the CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub> sample. Compared with TiO<sub>2</sub>, the absorption edge of CNâTiO<sub>2</sub> and CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub> has, respectively, shifted to 512 and 537 nm (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>e); therefore, the band gap energy of TiO<sub>2</sub>, CNâTiO<sub>2</sub>, and CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub> is 3.212, 2.871, and 2.721 eV, respectively (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/suppl_file/am1c10506_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>). This situation can also be observed in the XPS valence band spectrum; the band gap of three samples is 2.736, 2.58, and 2.079 eV, respectively (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>f). The narrowing of the band gap energy can be attributed to the doping of N, C, and OVs, which can reduce the transition energy of electrons from the VB to the CB, thereby causing a significant shift from the absorption edge to lower energy. These changes can also be reflected in the change in the color of the samples. The color of the TiO<sub>2</sub> sample is approximately white, while the color of CNâTiO<sub>2</sub> and CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub> samples are light grays (inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>e). The existence of N, C, and OVs is also illustrated by the Raman spectrum and EPR spectra (for details, please see Figures S8 and S9 in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/suppl_file/am1c10506_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>). This result is consistent with the UVâvis spectrum and XPS valence band spectrum. These results reveal the successfully introduced N, C atoms and OVs in the materials. The introduction of heterogeneous elements will help narrow the band gap, thereby improving the conductivity of CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub>. The BrunauerâEmmettâTeller (BET) specific surface areas of CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub> were 152.7 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>â1</sup>, which originates from many nano-scale holes and a shell composed of huge ultra-thin nanosheets (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/suppl_file/am1c10506_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>).</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/asset/images/medium/am1c10506_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/asset/images/large/am1c10506_0003.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. High-resolution XPS spectra of (a) N 1s, (b) C 1s, (c) O 1s, and (d) Ti 3d. (e) UVâvis absorption spectra. (f) XPS valence band spectra.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/asset/images/large/am1c10506_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c10506&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Sodium-ion half-cells were assembled to further explore the effects of dual doping on the behavior of sodium storage. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/suppl_file/am1c10506_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S11a,b</a> show the initial five CV curves of TiO<sub>2</sub>, CNâTiO<sub>2</sub>, and CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub> electrodes, the difference in their shapes comes from the effect of N, C, and OV co-doping with high concentration. Unlike the subsequent cycles, the first cathodic scan reveals a broad reduction peak at 1.13 V, which can be attributed to the formation of solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) layers. The peak at 0.43 V can be attributed to the reduction of Ti<sup>4+</sup> to Ti<sup>3+</sup>. The corresponding oxidation peak of Ti<sup>3+</sup> to Ti<sup>4+</sup> appears at 0.77 V in the anodic scan. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10" aria-label="reference 10">(10)</a> In the subsequent scan, the reduction peak shifted slightly to 0.69 V, and the oxidation peak shifted to 0.85 V. Besides, the last three scans overlap very well, indicating excellent electrochemical reversibility. It is worth mentioning that a series of small oxidation peaks appeared at 1.6â2.3 V, and the same was observed in the d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> curve derived from the differentiation of the capacityâvoltage curve at a low current density of 0.05 A g<sup>â1</sup> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/suppl_file/am1c10506_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12a</a>). For comparison, the CV curves and d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> curve of CNâTiO<sub>2</sub> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/suppl_file/am1c10506_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12b,c</a>) did not find such phenomena. This shows that there was a large amount of Ti<sup>3+</sup> oxidized to Ti<sup>4+</sup> in CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub> due to the existence of OVs. Through the synergistic effect of dual doping and OV strategy, the massive appearance of Ti<sup>3+</sup> during the hydrogenation process shows a positive effect on increasing the capacity. The GCD profiles of the CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub>, TiO<sub>2</sub> and CNâTiO<sub>2</sub> are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/suppl_file/am1c10506_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S13a,b</a>. The CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub> electrode delivers discharge and charge capacity of 575.5 and 297.9 mA h g<sup>â1</sup> at 0.05 A g<sup>â1</sup>, respectively, corresponding to an ICE of 51.8%, which is higher than that of TiO<sub>2</sub> (34.9%) and CNâTiO<sub>2</sub> (50.1%). The CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub> electrode delivers the highest capacity, keeping a specific capacity of 245 mA h g<sup>â1</sup> after 2000 cycles at a current density 0.1 A g<sup>â1</sup>. In contrast, the TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode only delivers a specific capacity of 40 mA h g<sup>â1</sup>, and the CNâTiO<sub>2</sub> electrode delivers a specific capacity of 147 mA h g<sup>â1</sup> due to the production of Ti<sup>3+</sup> in the annealing and N, C doping, but still lower than the CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub> electrode (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c). Obviously, the result indicated the importance of the dual doping strategy of N, C atoms and OVs in improving capacity and cycle stability. The chargeâdischarge curves at various current densities (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/suppl_file/am1c10506_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S14</a>) also show that CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub> has smaller polarization than TiO<sub>2</sub> and CNâTiO<sub>2</sub>, which further proves its better Na<sup>+</sup> conductivity and diffusion rate. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d shows the rate capabilities of three samples. Not surprisingly, CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub> still shows the best performance and it delivers specific capacities of 292, 242, 220, 192, 172, 152, 139, 124, and 97 mA h g<sup>â1</sup> at 0.05, 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, 3, 5, and 10 A g<sup>â1</sup>, respectively. The capacity returned to 233 mA h g<sup>â1</sup> when the current density returned to 0.05 A g<sup>â1</sup>, and there was almost no attenuation after 2000 cycles at a current density of 1 A g<sup>â1</sup>. The specific capacity of the CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub> electrode at every current density is higher than the other two electrodes, demonstrating that the CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub> electrode can tolerate various current densities. Not surprisingly. The long cycle performance of the CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub> and CNâTiO<sub>2</sub> electrode is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>e. The CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub> electrode can retain 91 mA h g<sup>â1</sup> after 10,000 cycles at 10 A g<sup>â1</sup>, which is higher than the CNâTiO<sub>2</sub> electrode. In addition, the cycle performance of the CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub> electrode at various current densities and high surface mass loading has also been tested (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/suppl_file/am1c10506_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S15âS19</a>). This excellent cycle performance and rate performance were superior to those of TiO<sub>2</sub>-base materials that have been reported (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/suppl_file/am1c10506_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>). All these phenomena indicate the high stability of the dual doping strategy during electrochemical cycling. The evolution of the crystal structure of CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub> during the first discharge/charge process was executed using ex-XRD, and the results are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/suppl_file/am1c10506_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S20</a>. It can be observed that the diffraction pattern can be well maintained during the whole charging and discharging processes, and no new diffraction peaks appear, which indicates that the intercalation of sodium ions occurs through the solid solution reaction mechanism without the phase change. During the discharge process, the intensity and position of the (001) diffraction peak hardly change until the voltage drops to 0.75 V. When it is further sodiated to 0.01 V, the intensity of the (001) diffraction peak gradually weakens. This change confirms the rearrangement of the crystal structure, but the degree of the lattice disorder is very weak, and the diffraction peaks are still clearly visible, which indicates that the long-range order of the atomic arrangement is well maintained, and further reveals that the pseudocapacitance mechanism of sodium ion intercalation does not cause a significant phase change. During the subsequent charging process, the intensity of the diffraction peak was significantly increased, showing that sodium ions reversibly intercalated/extracted from the host site. Ex situ XRD studies have confirmed that the intercalated pseudocapacitor sodium ion storage mechanism is conducive to maintaining the stability of the crystal structure and has highly reversible and long-lasting cycle performance. A similar phenomenon can also be observed in the reported literature. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a></div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/asset/images/medium/am1c10506_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/asset/images/large/am1c10506_0004.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) CV curves, (b) galvanostatic chargeâdischarge (GCD) curves at a current density of 0.05 A g<sup>â1</sup> for CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub>, (c) cycle performance at 0.1 A g<sup>â1</sup>, (d) rate performance, and (e) long cycle performance at 10 A g<sup>â1</sup> of the CNâTiO<sub>2</sub> and CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub> samples.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/asset/images/large/am1c10506_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c10506&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The charge storage behavior is further quantitatively analyzed by the CV curves with different sweep rates and is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/suppl_file/am1c10506_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S21</a>. Both the anode peak potential and the charging start potential gradually increase with the increase of the sweep rate due to the influence of polarization. The capacitive behavior of the electrodes has also been further analyzed. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/suppl_file/am1c10506_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S22</a> displays the b value, and the calculated b value is 0.921 and 0.912 for the cathodic peak and 0.924 and 0.875 for the anodic peak to CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub> and CNâTiO<sub>2</sub> for sweep rates lower than 2 mV s<sup>â1</sup>, indicating that most of the sodium ions are adsorbed on the surface or near the surface of the material. A large number of ultrathin nanosheets and a large specific surface area ensure the occurrence of surface pseudocapacitance behavior. Meanwhile, the <i>b</i> values of the CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub> are larger than those of CNâTiO<sub>2</sub>, suggesting that the introduction of OVs and Ti<sup>3+</sup> can enhance the pseudocapacitive charge storage response. For sweep rate above 2 mV s<sup>â1</sup>, the <i>b</i> value became smaller for all the cathodic and anodic peaks, which is caused by SEI resistance, active material resistance, and limited diffusion at a high sweep rate. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50 ref51'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50 ref51" aria-label="reference 50 and 51">(50,51)</a> The charge storage mechanism also can be judged by the relationship between the normalized capacity and the sweep rate (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/suppl_file/am1c10506_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S23a,b</a>). For the sweep rate above 2 mV s<sup>â1</sup>, the normalized capacity will decrease with the increase of the sweep rate, indicating the charge storage mechanism of diffusion control. The capacity depends on the sweep rate, indicating a surface diffusion process when the sweep rate is less than 2 mV s<sup>â1</sup>. The contribution of relative capacitance and diffusion control at a specific voltage can be obtained by analyzing the relationship between the scan rate and current response according to <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/suppl_file/am1c10506_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">eq S10</a> in the Supporting Information. As the sweep rate increases, the relative ratio of the capacitance contribution was also increasing, and when the sweep rate reaches 10 mV s<sup>â1</sup>, this ratio reaches 90% for CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub> and 85% for CNâTiO<sub>2</sub> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/suppl_file/am1c10506_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S23c,d</a>). Such high pseudocapacitance behavior is beneficial for a long cycle and high-rate performance.</div><div class="NLM_p">The SIFBs with the CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub> microspheres as the anode were assembled to further verify the excellent sodium storage performance of the CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub> electrode (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a). The SEM images, XRD pattern, and electrochemical test of NVP@C-BN electrodes were shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/suppl_file/am1c10506_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S24</a>. The NVP@C-BN//CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub> full cell presented average capacities of 173.7, 157.4, 146.9, 136.9, 123.3, 110.8, and 95.2 mA h g<sup>â1</sup> at current densities of 0.05, 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, and 5 A g<sup>â1</sup>, respectively (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b), and the shapes of the charge/discharge profiles retained well with current density increased (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c). Besides, the long cycle of SIFBs was tested at a current density of 1 A g<sup>â1</sup>, which exhibited a specific capacity of 115 mA h g<sup>â1</sup> after 2000 cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d,e). Such excellent kinetic matching benefits from the outstanding electrochemical performance of the cathode and anode materials.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/asset/images/medium/am1c10506_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/asset/images/large/am1c10506_0005.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) Schematic illustration for SIFBs, (b) chargeâdischarge curves at various current densities, (c) rate performance, (d) GCD curves, and (e) cycle performance at a current density of 1 A g<sup>â1</sup> of the SIFBs.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/asset/images/large/am1c10506_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c10506&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The SICs were assembled with CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub> as the anode and AC as the cathode. During the charging process, Na<sup>+</sup> is embedded in the CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub> electrode and the surface interface area from the electrolyte, ClO<sup>â</sup> is adsorbed on AC to form an electric double layer to achieve charge balance, and the discharge process is just the opposite (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a). The SEM images, XRD pattern, and electrochemical test of AC electrodes are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/suppl_file/am1c10506_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S25</a>. The good rate and long cycle performance ensure the rapid adsorption and desorption of sodium ions. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/suppl_file/am1c10506_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S26</a> show the CV curves of successful SICs at various sweep rates, which all have similar shapes, proving good electrochemical reversibility. The small peak at 3.5â4.0 V indicated the mixed charge storage mechanism of Faraday and non-Faraday reactions. A near ideal capacitive behavior can be observed from the near-linear chargeâdischarge curves, which once again reveals the interface electrochemical reaction of fast energy storage kinetics (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c). The cycle performance of SICs was screened via the comparison of the cycle performances at different mass ratios. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/suppl_file/am1c10506_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S27</a>, the results indicate that the best electrochemical performance is shown when the mass ratio of positive and anode active material is 4:1. As a result, the SICs present an average energy density of 174.2 and 51.2 W h kg<sup>â1</sup> with a power density of 125 and 12,500 W kg<sup>â1</sup> (at current densities of 0.05 and 5.0 A g<sup>â1</sup>, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d). The Ragone plots of CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub>//AC SICs verify the strong advantages compared to other commercial capacitors, as well as the expectation for wider applications in the future (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/suppl_file/am1c10506_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S28</a>). The SICs also show outstanding cycle performance at 1 A g<sup>â1</sup>, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>e, which can still maintain a capacity retention rate of 74.2% after 8000 cycles. Considering the cost advantage, TiO<sub>2</sub>-base SICs are expected to be used in large-scale fast energy storage devices in the future.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/asset/images/medium/am1c10506_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/asset/images/large/am1c10506_0006.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) Schematic illustration for SIC device. (b) CV curves, (c) voltageâtime curves at various current densities, (d) rate performance, and (e) long cycling performance of the SICs (inset: CP curves at different states).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/asset/images/large/am1c10506_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c10506&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub> electrode provides the highest capacity and the longest cycle life among all the tested samples, implying the powerful effect of the dual doping strategy in improving the sodium storage capacity of the TiO<sub>2</sub> anode. The microstructure analysis of the electrode cycled further verified extraordinary cycling stability. The CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub> electrode almost maintained its original structure after 100 cycles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/suppl_file/am1c10506_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S29</a>), and the ultra-thin nanosheets are still clearly visible (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a). The SEI film on the electrode surface gradually stabilizes as the cycle progresses, and finally, a uniform and dense layer were formed (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>b,c), indicating that there is no unnecessary side reaction between the electrode and the electrolyte, which is extremely beneficial for long-cycle performance. The TiO<sub>2</sub> (110) crystal plane can be still seen by the HRTEM (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>d). The EDS mapping proves that Ti, O, C, and N elements are uniformly distributed in the material surface (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>e). Besides, the XRD patterns with different cycles also confirmed that the crystalline structure of TiO<sub>2</sub> remains intact (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>f). The doping of N, C, and OVs enhance the electron/ion dynamics and provide more adsorption sites for sodium ions in the CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub> sample, which is beneficial to increase capacity and maintain cycling stability. These effects all contribute to the pseudocapacitive behavior of sodium ion insertion in CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub> samples, thereby ensuring outstanding electrochemical performance.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/asset/images/medium/am1c10506_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/asset/images/large/am1c10506_0007.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. (aâc) TEM images, (d) HRTEM image (inset: corresponding the FFT pattern), and (e) HADDFâSTEM mapping of CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub> after 100 cycles (scale bar: 100 nm). (f) XRD patterns of CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub> after different cycles.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/asset/images/large/am1c10506_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c10506&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>aâc shows three different doping models and the effect of doping on the sodium storage behavior of TiO<sub>2</sub>. N and C co-doping enhances the adsorption of TiO<sub>2</sub> to sodium ions. Furthermore, the doping of high-concentration OVs reduces the forbidden band and enhances electronic conductivity. The synergistic strategy of dual doping effectively improves the sodium storage ability of TiO<sub>2</sub>. The adsorption energy (<i>E</i><sub>ads</sub>) of sodium ions is further calculated using the DFT method. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>d shows the geometrically optimized structure diagram, and the value of the <i>E</i><sub>ads</sub> of sodium ion intercalated into TiO<sub>2</sub>, CNâTiO<sub>2</sub>, and CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub>, indicating that sodium ions are more easily embedded in CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub>. The <i>E</i><sub>ads</sub> of sodium ions on single-doped TiO<sub>2</sub> was also calculated to verify the influence of single doping on the sodium storage behavior of TiO<sub>2</sub> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/suppl_file/am1c10506_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S30</a>). The single-atom doping enhances the adsorption of sodium ions. Ab initio first principles were used to further evaluate the electronic structure of the samples by the density of states (DOS). The doped N, C and OVs often cause unformed electrons in the crystal, resulting in the introduction of intermediate bands (IBs) and further affecting the band gap. The TDOS and PDOS of TiO<sub>2</sub>, CNâTiO<sub>2</sub>, and CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub> are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>e. As described, the VB and CB of TiO<sub>2</sub> originates from O 2p and Ti 3d, respectively. Unlike TiO<sub>2</sub>, the presence of IBs is observed in the other DOS patterns, and the IBs are mainly derived from the hybridization of O 2p and Ti 3d orbitals. These IBs could act as a springboard for electronic transitions and produce a positive impact for the transition of electrons from the VB to the CB. The quantitative analysis shows the band gap of CNâTiO<sub>2</sub> and CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub> decrease from 2.697 to 0.959 and 0.369 eV, respectively, and this value is also much smaller than the band gap of single-atom doping TiO<sub>2</sub> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/suppl_file/am1c10506_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S31</a>). Therefore, as confirmed by the results of the XPS valence band spectrum, KubelkaâMunk plot, and DFT calculations, the dual doping strategy can effectively adjust the electronic structure of TiO<sub>2</sub> with a narrower band gap, thereby increasing the electronic conductivity. Excellent cycling and rate performance mainly rely on fast ion/electron kinetics. The galvanostatic intermittent titration technique test (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/suppl_file/am1c10506_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S32</a>) and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy method test (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/suppl_file/am1c10506_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S33</a>) indicated that the CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub> electrode has a faster ion diffusion coefficient and smaller polarization. The diffusion barriers for sodium-ion migration were calculated using the climbing NEB method (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>f). The diffusion barrier of sodium ions in TiO<sub>2</sub>, CNâTiO<sub>2</sub>, and CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub> electrode is 0.542, 0.267, and 0.137 eV, respectively. The diffusion barrier of sodium ions in single-doped TiO<sub>2</sub> is also calculated, and the results still prove that the synergy of the dual doping strategy has a positive effect on reducing the sodium-ion diffusion barrier (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/suppl_file/am1c10506_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S34</a>). It provides qualitative insights into the excellent kinetic performance of CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub>. These results show that the N, C, and OV doping redistributes the internal charges of the crystal. Under the synergistic effect of morphology and heterogeneous element doping, it significantly accelerates the diffusion kinetics, reduces the diffusion barrier of sodium ions, and improves the interface dynamics.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/asset/images/medium/am1c10506_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/asset/images/large/am1c10506_0008.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. Illustration of the Na<sup>+</sup> and e<sup>â</sup> on various TiO<sub>2</sub>: (a) TiO<sub>2</sub>, (b) CNâTiO<sub>2</sub>, and (c) CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub>. (d) Absorption energy and optimized geometries of Na<sup>+</sup> on TiO<sub>2</sub>, CNâTiO<sub>2</sub>, and CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub>. (e) Total/partial densities of states (TDOS/PDOS) of TiO<sub>2</sub>, CNâTiO<sub>2</sub>, and CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub>. (f) Diffusion barrier of Na<sup>+</sup> in various TiO<sub>2</sub>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/asset/images/large/am1c10506_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c10506&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i4">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, N, C, and OV co-doped TiO<sub>2</sub> microspheres constructed from ultrathin nanosheets were prepared through one-step hydrothermal and hydrogen treatment. Ultra-thin nanosheets provide more and faster sodium ion movement channels, increase the contact area between the electrode and the electrolyte, and the N, C, and OVs co-doped enhance the electronic/ionic dynamics and reduce the diffusion barrier. Therefore, the CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub> electrode delivers a high specific capacity (245 mA h g<sup>â1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>â1</sup>), excellent cycling stability (91 mA h g<sup>â1</sup> for 10,000 cycles at 10 A g<sup>â1</sup> for the half-cell, 115 mA h g<sup>â1</sup> for 2000 cycles at 1 A g<sup>â1</sup> for SIFBs), high energy and power density (174.2 W h kg<sup>â1</sup> at 125 W kg<sup>â1</sup>, 51.2 W h kg<sup>â1</sup> at 12,500 W kg<sup>â1</sup> for SICs), and durable cycling ability (55 W h kg<sup>â1</sup> for 8000 cycles at 2500 W kg<sup>â1</sup> for SICs). Besides, the DFT calculation also further proves that the dual doping and OV synergy strategy promotes the electrical conductivity, lowers the sodiation energy barrier, and facilitates the sodium-ion diffusion kinetics. This work emphasizes the importance of defect engineering in improving material conductivity and electrode cycling stability for possible practical applications shortly.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i5">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec4_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6"> Material Preparation</h3><div class="NLM_p last">In a typical procedure, 0.65 mL diethylenetriamine (DETA, 99%) and 2 mL titanium(IV) isopropoxide (95%) were, respectively, added to 22.5 mL isopropyl alcohol (99.5%) and sonicated for 10 min. Then, the two solutions were mixed and continuously stirred for 20 min. Finally, the above-mixed solution was transferred to an autoclave with a capacity of 100 mL and placed in an oven at 200 Â°C for 24 h. The precipitate was separated through centrifugation, and washing with deionized water (DI) and ethanol several times after cooling to room temperature, TiO<sub>2</sub> was obtained. To obtain CNOvâTiO<sub>2</sub>, the pre-prepared sample was annealed at 500 Â°C for 2 h in an Ar/H<sub>2</sub> (95%/5%) atmosphere. CNâTiO<sub>2</sub> was obtained and annealed at 500 Â°C for 2 h in the Ar atmosphere. Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C-BN (NVP@C-BN) was prepared according to previous literature reports. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref52'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52" aria-label="reference 52">(52)</a> AC was purchased from YP-80F of Japan Kuraray Company.</div></div><div id="sec4_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7"> Material Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The structure and morphology of all samples were characterized on a LabX XRD-6000, field-emission scanning electron microscopy (HITACHI SU-8010), and transmission electron microscopy (JEOL-2100F instrument). XPS measurements, Raman spectra, electron spin-resonance spectroscopy, and BET surface areas results were collected.</div></div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i8">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.1c10506?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.1c10506</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Details of material characterization, electrochemical measurements, and theoretical calculation; additional characterization and electrochemical performance of TiO<sub>2</sub> and CNâTiO<sub>2</sub>; and table on the comparison of TiO<sub>2</sub>-based anode material performance of various materials (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/suppl_file/am1c10506_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c10506/suppl_file/am1c10506_si_001.pdf">am1c10506_si_001.pdf (3.06 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.1c10506" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrode materials for rechargeable sodium-ion batteries: potential alternatives to current lithium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kim, Sung-Wook; Seo, Dong-Hwa; Ma, Xiaohua; Ceder, Gerbrand; Kang, Kisuk</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">2</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">710-721</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADEMBC</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1614-6840</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Lithium (Li)-ion batteries (LIB) have governed the current worldwide rechargeable battery market due to their outstanding energy and power capability.  In particular, the LIB's role in enabling elec. vehicles (EVs) has been highlighted to replace the current oil-driven vehicles in order to reduce the usage of oil resources and generation of CO2 gases.  Unlike Li, sodium is one of the more abundant elements on Earth and exhibits similar chem. properties to Li, indicating that Na chem. could be applied to a similar battery system.  In the 1970s-80s, both Na-ion and Li-ion electrodes were investigated, but the higher energy d. of Li-ion cells made them more applicable to small, portable electronic devices, and research efforts for rechargeable batteries have been mainly concd. on LIB since then.  Recently, research interest in Na-ion batteries (NIB) has been resurrected, driven by new applications with requirements different from those in portable electronics, and to address the concern on Li abundance.  In this article, both neg. and pos. electrode materials in NIB are briefly reviewed.  While the voltage is generally lower and the vol. change upon Na removal or insertion is larger for Na-intercalation electrodes, compared to their Li equiv., the power capability can vary depending on the crystal structures.  It is concluded that cost-effective NIB can partially replace LIB, but requires further investigation and improvement.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGonnrfiSUpaRrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lixgO5Y7lJysQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XhtVOrtLjF&amp;md5=485b20393bb8876b2f69ca708aeba606</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1039/c9ta09643d"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Han, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Si, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chai, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jiao, L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">N-Doped CoSb@C Nanofibers as a Self-Supporting Anode for High-Performance K-Ion and Na-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">N-doped CoSb@C nanofibers as a self-supporting anode for high-performance K-ion and Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Han, Jun; Zhu, Kunjie; Liu, Pei; Si, Yuchang; Chai, Yujun; Jiao, Lifang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Herein, the prepn. of CoSb nanoparticles embedded in carbon nanofibers with a raised surface (N-doped CoSb@C nanofibers) via the electrospinning method is reported for the first time.  The distinct structure facilitates the pseudocapacitive behaviors, which is the key to obtain fast kinetics and long cyclability.  N and Co elements can not only optimize the electronic configuration but also buffer the vol. changes of the material during cycling.  For potassium-ion batteries, the capacity maintains 250 mA h g-1 after 500 cycles at 1 A g-1, and for sodium-ion batteries, the capacity keeps 413 mA h g-1 after 1000 cycles at 1 A g-1.  Thus, this work makes a great contribution towards exploring high-capacity anodes for practical potassium-ion batteries and sodium-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoQKX1IpNRVY7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lixgO5Y7lJysQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXhvFymsb%252FF&amp;md5=a0a468cd018aa0125c2daa9ff77dfbc4</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.34133/2019/6930294"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xia, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xie, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gu, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tu, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">High Capacity and Superior Rate Performances Coexisting in Carbon-Based Sodium-Ion Battery Anode</span>. <i>Research</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>2019</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">6930294</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High capacity and superior rate performances coexisting in carbon-based sodium-ion battery anode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Yuqian; Zhang, Liyuan; Wang, Xiuli; Xia, Xinhui; Xie, Dong; Gu, Changdong; Tu, Jiangping</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Research (Washington, DC, United States)</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Association for the Advancement of Science</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Amorphous carbon is considered as a prospective and serviceable anode for the storage of sodium.  In this contribution, we illuminate the transformation rule of defect/void ratio and the restrictive relation between specifc capacity and rate capability.  Inspired by this mechanism, ratio of plateau/slope capacity is regulated via temp.-control pyrolysis.  Moreover, pore-forming reaction is induced to create defects, open up the isolated voids, and build fast ion channels to further enhance the capacity and rate ability.  Numerous fast ion channels, high ion-electron cond., and abundant defects lead the designed porous hard carbon/Co3O4 anode to realize a high specifc capacity, prolonged circulation ability, and enhanced capacity at high rates.  Tis research deepens the comprehension of sodium storage behavior and proposes a fabrication approach to achieve high performance carbonaceous anodes for sodium-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp_0VZqb8c_ZbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lghdpX7JmZmNg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXit1Sgs7vM&amp;md5=bc3a9393d804c3271239d5cfd700fb90</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1021/cr500192f"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yabuuchi, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kubota, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dahbi, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Komaba, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Research Development on Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Chem.                         cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-storage behavior of electron-rich element-doped amorphous carbon</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Yuqian; Zhang, Liyuan; Wang, Xiuli; Xia, Xinhui; Xie, Dong; Gu, Changdong; Tu, Jiangping</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Applied Physics Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">8</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">011402</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">APRPG5</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1931-9401</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Institute of Physics</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  In recent years, sodium ion batteries (SIBs) have been widely investigated due to limited lithium resources.  Hence, it is of great importance to develop suitable anodes for SIBs.  In recent works, doped amorphous carbon has been considered a prospective and serviceable anode for the storage of sodium.  In this study, sodium-storage behavior in electron-rich, element-modified, amorphous carbon is addressed, considering N and P.  The affinity of N-doped amorphous carbon is identified by calcg. the electron distributions of the N-doped structures.  Furthermore, the adsorption energies of sodium in the P-doped amorphous carbon systems are analyzed to elucidate the storage behavior of doping.  From the above anal., the internal structure of co-doped carbon is characterized and pyrrolic N and P-O structures reveal excellent sodium-storage performance.  Consequently, hydrothermal treatment is designed to build the precursor of the required P-O structure.  Based on the sodium-storage theory, a carbon anode doped with dual electron-rich elements is synthesized successfully, which shows enhanced electrochem. performances in terms of cycle life and capacity in batteries.  As a result, these research results fill the theor. gap of the sodium-storage behavior of electron-rich, element-doped, amorphous carbon and provide the exptl. basis for its application.                         cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium Storage Behavior in Natural Graphite using Ether-based Electrolyte Systems</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kim, Haegyeom; Hong, Jihyun; Park, Young-Uk; Kim, Jinsoo; Hwang, Insang; Kang, Kisuk</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">This work reports that natural graphite is capable of Na insertion and extn. with a remarkable reversibility using ether-based electrolytes.  Natural graphite (the most well-known anode material for Li-ion batteries) has been barely studied as a suitable anode for Na rechargeable batteries due to the lack of Na intercalation capability.  Herein, graphite is not only capable of Na intercalation but also exhibits outstanding performance as an anode for Na-ion batteries.  The graphite anode delivers a reversible capacity of â150 mA-h/g with a cycle stability for 2500 cycles, and more than 75 mA-h/g at 10 A/g despite its micrometer-size (â100 Î¼m).  An Na storage mechanism in graphite, where Na+-solvent co-intercalation occurs combined with partial pseudocapacitive behaviors, is shown.  It is demonstrated that the electrolyte solvent species significantly affect the electrochem. properties, not only rate capability but also redox potential.  The feasibility of graphite in a Na full cell is also confirmed in conjunction with the Na1.5VPO4.8F0.7 cathode, delivering an energy of â120 W-h/kg while maintaining â70% of the initial capacity after 250 cycles.  This exceptional behavior of natural graphite promises new avenues for the development of cost-effective and reliable Na-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpH2c5ZOoUcVbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lghdpX7JmZmNg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhvFGiurrN&amp;md5=0bb4c36145b59a3b9727155eca307655</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1360/n972018-00790"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">High-Temperature Treatment Induced Carbon Anode with Ultrahigh Na Storage Capacity at Low-Voltage Plateau</span>. <i>Sci.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (NIBs) show great prospect on the energy storage applications benefiting from their low cost and the abundant Na resources despite the expected lower energy d. compared with lithium-ion batteries (LIBs).                         cas_cit9R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">9</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Pseudocapacitive Sodium Storage in Mesoporous Single-Crystal-like TiO2-Graphene Nanocomposite Enables High-Performance Sodium-Ion Capacitors</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Le, Zaiyuan; Liu, Fang; Nie, Ping; Li, Xinru; Liu, Xiaoyan; Bian, Zhenfeng; Chen, Gen; Wu, Hao Bin; Lu, Yunfeng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion capacitors can potentially combine the virtues of high power capability of conventional electrochem. capacitors and high energy d. of batteries.  However, the lack of high-performance electrode materials has been the major challenge of sodium-based energy storage devices.  We report a microwave-assisted synthesis of single-crystal-like anatase TiO2 mesocages anchored on graphene as a sodium storage material.  The architecture of the nanocomposite results in pseudocapacitive charge storage behavior with fast kinetics, high reversibility, and negligible degrdn. to the micro/nanostructure.  The nanocomposite delivers a high capacity of 268 mAh/g at 0.2 C, which remains 126 mAh/g at 10 C for over 18,000 cycles.  Coupling with a C-based cathode, a full cell of sodium-ion capacitor successfully demonstrates a high energy d. of 64.2 Wh/Kg at 56.3 W/Kg and 25.8 Wh/Kg at 1357 W/Kg, as well as an ultralong lifespan of 10,000 cycles with over 90% of capacity retention.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrefz1bcMVcvLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgvjuIIZzFmBA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXktV2mtrY%253D&amp;md5=d64d5206ca24d3eb81b3db6738efa485</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit10" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201701222"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, Y.
